<meta name="DC.Description" content="The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley provides a robust framework to better understand Poaceae evolution and a substantial basis for functional genomics of crop species with a large genome. The expansion of stress-related gene families in Tibetan hulless barley implies that it could be considered as an invaluable gene resource aiding stress tolerance improvement in Triticeae crops. Genome resequencing revealed extensive genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasm and divergence to sequenced barley genomes from other geographical regions. Investigation of genome-wide selection footprints demonstrated an adaptive correlation of genes under selection with extensive stressful environmental variables. These results reveal insights into the adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley to harsh environments on the highland and will facilitate future genetic improvement of crops." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;The Tibetan hulless barley (&lt;i&gt;Hordeum vulgare&lt;/i&gt; L. var. &lt;i&gt;nudum&lt;/i&gt;), also called “Qingke” in Chinese and “&lt;i&gt;Ne&lt;/i&gt;” in Tibetan, is the staple food for Tibetans and an important livestock feed in the Tibetan Plateau. The diploid nature and adaptation to diverse environments of the highland give it unique resources for genetic research and crop improvement. Here we produced a 3.89-Gb draft assembly of Tibetan hulless barley with 36,151 predicted protein-coding genes. Comparative analyses revealed the divergence times and synteny between barley and other representative Poaceae genomes. The expansion of the gene family related to stress responses was found in Tibetan hulless barley. Resequencing of 10 barley accessions uncovered high levels of genetic variation in Tibetan wild barley and genetic divergence between Tibetan and non-Tibetan barley genomes. Selective sweep analyses demonstrate adaptive correlations of genes under selection with extensive environmental variables. Our results not only construct a genomic framework for crop improvement but also provide evolutionary insights of highland adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley provides a robust framework to better understand Poaceae evolution and a substantial basis for functional genomics of crop species with a large genome. The expansion of stress-related gene families in Tibetan hulless barley implies that it could be considered as an invaluable gene resource aiding stress tolerance improvement in Triticeae crops. Genome resequencing revealed extensive genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasm and divergence to sequenced barley genomes from other geographical regions. Investigation of genome-wide selection footprints demonstrated an adaptive correlation of genes under selection with extensive stressful environmental variables. These results reveal insights into the adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley to harsh environments on the highland and will facilitate future genetic improvement of crops.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/highwire/pnas/112/4.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley provides a robust framework to better understand Poaceae evolution and a substantial basis for functional genomics of crop species with a large genome. The expansion of stress-related gene families in Tibetan hulless barley implies that it could be considered as an invaluable gene resource aiding stress tolerance improvement in Triticeae crops. Genome resequencing revealed extensive genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasm and divergence to sequenced barley genomes from other geographical regions. Investigation of genome-wide selection footprints demonstrated an adaptive correlation of genes under selection with extensive stressful environmental variables. These results reveal insights into the adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley to harsh environments on the highland and will facilitate future genetic improvement of crops." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley provides a robust framework to better understand Poaceae evolution and a substantial basis for functional genomics of crop species with a large genome. The expansion of stress-related gene families in Tibetan hulless barley implies that it could be considered as an invaluable gene resource aiding stress tolerance improvement in Triticeae crops. Genome resequencing revealed extensive genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasm and divergence to sequenced barley genomes from other geographical regions. Investigation of genome-wide selection footprints demonstrated an adaptive correlation of genes under selection with extensive stressful environmental variables. These results reveal insights into the adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley to harsh environments on the highland and will facilitate future genetic improvement of crops." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="165069" id="node165069" data-pisa="pnas;112/4/1095" data-pisa-master="pnas;1423628112" data-apath="/pnas/112/4/1095.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">

China</span>;</div></span></div><ul class="author-tooltip-find-more"><li class="author-tooltip-gs-link first"><a href="/lookup/google-scholar?link_type=googlescholar&amp;gs_type=author&amp;author%5B0%5D=Maoqun%2BYu%2B" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on Google Scholar</a></li>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-5">The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley provides a robust framework to better understand Poaceae evolution and a substantial basis for functional genomics of crop species with a large genome. The expansion of stress-related gene families in Tibetan hulless barley implies that it could be considered as an invaluable gene resource aiding stress tolerance improvement in Triticeae crops. Genome resequencing revealed extensive genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasm and divergence to sequenced barley genomes from other geographical regions. Investigation of genome-wide selection footprints demonstrated an adaptive correlation of genes under selection with extensive stressful environmental variables. These results reveal insights into the adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley to harsh environments on the highland and will facilitate future genetic improvement of crops.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-6">The Tibetan hulless barley (<em>Hordeum vulgare</em> L. var. <em>nudum</em>), also called “Qingke” in Chinese and “<em>Ne</em>” in Tibetan, is the staple food for Tibetans and an important livestock feed in the Tibetan Plateau. The diploid nature and adaptation to diverse environments of the highland give it unique resources for genetic research and crop improvement. Here we produced a 3.89-Gb draft assembly of Tibetan hulless barley with 36,151 predicted protein-coding genes. Comparative analyses revealed the divergence times and synteny between barley and other representative Poaceae genomes. The expansion of the gene family related to stress responses was found in Tibetan hulless barley. Resequencing of 10 barley accessions uncovered high levels of genetic variation in Tibetan wild barley and genetic divergence between Tibetan and non-Tibetan barley genomes. Selective sweep analyses demonstrate adaptive correlations of genes under selection with extensive environmental variables. Our results not only construct a genomic framework for crop improvement but also provide evolutionary insights of highland adaptation of Tibetan hulless barley.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/tibetan-hulless-barley" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-tibetan-hulless-barley" rel="nofollow">Tibetan hulless barley</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/triticeae-evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-triticeae-evolution" rel="nofollow">Triticeae evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/genetic-diversity" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-genetic-diversity" rel="nofollow">genetic diversity</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/adaptation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-adaptation" rel="nofollow">adaptation</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/selective-sweep" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-selective-sweep" rel="nofollow">selective sweep</a></li></ul><p id="p-7" class="flushleft">Genome sequences provide a substantial basis for understanding the biological essences of crops associated with biologically and economically essential traits. Therefore, decoding a genome will benefit crop development to feed the increasing demand for food brought by climate change and population growth. The Triticeae species, such as wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum</em>, 2<em>n</em> = 6x = 42) and barley (<em>Hordeum vulgare</em>, 2<em>n</em> = 2x = 14), which account for &gt;30% of cereal production worldwide, are essential food and forage resources (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org" class="in-nw">faostat.fao.org</a>). International efforts have been launched to decipher their genomes, and dramatic breakthroughs have been achieved on the reference genome of chromosome 3B (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>), whole-genome sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>), and in-depth phylogenetic and transcriptome analyses (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>) of hexaploid wheat, as well as generations of draft genome sequences (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>, <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>) and construction of a physical map of its diploid A-genome (<em>Triticum urartu</em>, 2<em>n</em> = 2x = 14) and D-genome (<em>Aegilops tauschii</em>, 2<em>n</em> = 2x = 14) progenitors (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a><a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-7"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>).</p><p id="p-8">Barley, as one of the earliest domesticated crops and the world’s fourth most abundant cereal, is widely used in the brewing industry as a stock feed and in potential healthy food products (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>, <a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). As a diploid inbreeder, barley has long been considered as a genetic model for cereal crops in Triticeae. Recently, the International Barley Sequencing Consortium (IBSC) built a barley physical map of 4.98 giga base pairs (Gb), with more than 3.90 Gb anchored to a high-resolution genetic map (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Supported by DNA and deep RNA sequence data, 26,159 “high-confidence” genes were identified. Furthermore, extensive single-nucleotide variation was found by survey sequencing of diverse barley accessions. These achievements provide unprecedented insight into the barley genome.</p><p id="p-9">Compared with common cultivated barley with covered caryopsis, hulless barley (<em>H. vulgare</em> L. var. <em>nudum</em>), with naked caryopsis, is mainly cultivated in Tibet and its vicinity, which is one of the domestication and diversity centers for cultivated barley (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a><a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-13"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-14"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-15"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). The adaptation to extreme environmental conditions in high altitudes made it the staple food for Tibetans beginning at least 3,500–4,000 years ago (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). It continues to be the predominant crop in Tibet, occupying ∼70% of crop lands (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>). To facilitate the genetic development and gene identification of Tibetan hulless barley, we built the draft genome sequences of <em>Lasa Goumang</em>, a landrace of Tibetan hulless barley. Genome comparative analyses make deeper insights into the evolution among Poaceae species. Comparison analyses between Tibetan barleys and non-Tibetan barleys reveal genes associated with natural selections, which will help in the understanding of the adaptation biology of the barleys in the Tibetan Plateau.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Genome Assembly and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-10">We used a whole-genome shotgun strategy to sequence a series of libraries from 250 base pairs (bp) to 40 kilobase pairs (kb) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>). A total of 797 Gb of high-quality sequence data were generated, which has an approximate 178-fold depth of the estimated genome size of 4.48 Gb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2 and Fig. S2</a>). We built a de novo assembly of 3.89 Gb, with contig and scaffold N50 lengths of 18.07 kb and 242 kb, respectively (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S2–S4 and Tables S3 and S4</a>). The assembly showed high genome coverage and single-base accuracy evaluated by bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) sequences and RNA-seq data (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5 and Tables S5–S7</a>). We also anchored 28,374 scaffolds, representing 89.4% of the genome, onto seven chromosomes using the integrated and ordered physical and genetic map of cultivated barley (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S8 and S9</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/482148/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/482148/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-11" class="first-child">Statistics of the draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Overview of Tibetan hulless barley genome. (Track c) LTR retro-transposon (%) per 10 Mb&#x2014;min., 0; max., 100. (Track d) Synteny with the B. distachyon genome. (Track e) Tibetan hulless barley chromosomes with centromeres marked as black bands. (Track f) Syntenic blocks within and between chromosomes." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-997626578" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Overview of Tibetan hulless barley genome. (Track c) LTR retro-transposon (%) per 10 Mb&#x2014;min., 0; max., 100. (Track d) Synteny with the &lt;em&gt;B. distachyon&lt;/em&gt; genome. (Track e) Tibetan hulless barley chromosomes with centromeres marked as black bands. (Track f) Syntenic blocks within and between chromosomes.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F1.medium.gif" width="430" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F1.medium.gif" width="430" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/482135" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Overview of Tibetan hulless barley genome. (Track a) Gene regions of cultivated barley cv. Morex (%) per 10 Mb—min., 0; max., 10. (Track b) Gene regions of Tibetan hulless barley (%) per 10 Mb—min., 0; max., 10. (Track c) LTR retro-transposon (%) per 10 Mb—min., 0; max., 100. (Track d) Synteny with the <em>B. distachyon</em> genome. (Track e) Tibetan hulless barley chromosomes with centromeres marked as black bands. (Track f) Syntenic blocks within and between chromosomes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-13">Using a combination of evidence-based and de novo approaches, ∼81.4% of our assembly were identified as repetitive elements (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>), which is similar to those of Morex (<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>) and maize (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S10–S12</a>). In contrast to <em>T. urartu</em> and <em>Ae. tauschii</em>, the long terminal retrotransposons (LTRs) of Tibetan hulless barley contribute 68.3% to the whole genome, which is consistent with those obtained from gene-bearing BACs (<a id="xref-ref-11-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). The extent of divergence shows that the transposable element (TE) repeats were recently produced or anciently produced by transposition (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). We annotated 36,151 protein-coding genes combining various strategies of ab initio, homology, and transcriptome predictions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7 and Tables S13–S16</a>) as well as the full-length cDNA of barley (<a id="xref-ref-11-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), which is comparable with those in <em>T. urartu</em> (34,879) (<a id="xref-ref-6-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>) and <em>Ae. tauschii</em> (34,498) (<a id="xref-ref-7-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). As observed, the gene density is relatively lower surrounding the centromeres, where the repetitive contents are inversely higher, indicating the widely scattered gene deserts in the Tibetan hulless barley genome. Of the genes, 93.9% (33,928) were located on chromosomes, and 82.2% (29,730) were functionally annotated by multiple databases (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S17 and S18</a>).</p><p id="p-14">We mapped the genome data of barley accessions sequenced by IBSC to the Tibetan hulless barley genome including Morex, Bowman, Barke, <em>Hordeum spontaneum</em>, Haruna Nijo, and Igri. The coverage rates range from 82.35% to 94.96% (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S19</a>). There was no obvious difference in coverage rates across the seven chromosomes, which implies no preference of homogeny on different chromosomes. Moreover, we identified 113 megabase pairs (Mb) and 288 Mb of specific sequences for Morex (5.35%) and Tibetan hulless barley (7.90%), respectively (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S20</a>). More than 4,500 genes are involved in Tibetan hulless barley-specific sequences. The overrepresenting KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) pathways are flavonoid biosynthesis, stilbenoid, diarylheptanoid and gingerol biosynthesis, and plant hormone signal transduction (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S21</a>). By comparison to 26,159 high-confidence gene sets of the Morex genome (<a id="xref-ref-11-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), we identified 22,673 reciprocal best hits, in which 7,224 (31.86%) gene pairs had identical sequences and 17,840 (78.68%) showed protein similarity higher than 95% (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8<em>A</em></a>). Most of these gene pairs have similar lengths of coding DNA sequence (CDS) and gene body (CDS+intron), except that about 10.97% of genes in Tibetan hulless barley exhibited much longer gene body lengths than those in Morex (ratio, &gt;3.0) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8<em>B</em></a>). These results indicate the level of genomic divergence between the two species.</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Evolutionary Characteristics.</h3><p id="p-15">We estimated the divergence times of Tibetan hulless barley from other nine Poaceae species and three dicotyledons with 153 single-copy genes identified among these species (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S9 and S10 and Tables S22–S24</a>). It showed that barley was separated from <em>Ae. tauschii</em>, <em>T. urartu</em>, and <em>T. aestivum</em> ∼17 million years ago (Mya), using the 95% credibility interval, and this divergence could have occurred ∼28.6–8.8 Mya (the same below) and speciated from <em>Brachypodium distachyon</em>, <em>Phyllostachys heterocycla</em>, and <em>Oryza sativa</em> ∼48 (74.2–27.0) Mya, ∼64(95.8–37.3) Mya, and ∼71(105.3–41.8) Mya, respectively. It was estimated that <em>Ae. tauschii</em> diverged from <em>T. urartu</em> ∼10 (17.6–5.5) Mya. To better understand Triticeae evolution at the genome level, we also analyzed the whole-genome duplication (WGD) event and synteny of Tibetan hulless barley and sequenced Triticeae species. A total of 1,320 syntenic blocks, comprising 10,502 paralogous gene pairs, in Tibetan hulless barley uncovered an ancient WGD, which is common to other Poaceae genomes (<a id="xref-ref-7-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>). In addition, 637 and 486 large syntenic blocks, comprising 6,209 and 4,346 orthologous gene pairs, were identified between barley and <em>Ae. tauschii</em>, and barley and <em>T. urartu</em>, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11–S14 and Table S25</a>). The orthologous relationship between barley and <em>O. sativa</em> chromosomes provides evidence that at least four major nested chromosome fusions occurred in barley from its intermediate ancestor (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S15</a>). The regions outside these synteny blocks were rich in LTR repeats (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figs. 1</a> and <a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">2<em>C</em></a>), which may be caused by retrotransposition after speciation.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogeny, WGD, and gene families of Tibetan hulless barley compared with other plant genomes. (A) The divergence time tree estimated on fourfold degenerate sites of single-copy orthologous genes. (B) WGD and divergence of Tibetan hulless barley indicated by 4DTv (deviation at the fourfold degenerate third codon position) distribution. (C) Chromosomal syntenic relationship between Tibetan hulless barley and wheat A- and D-genome progenitors. (D) Gene families of H. vulgare, T. urartu, T. aestivum, Ae.tauschii, and B. distachyon. (E) Gene families of Ehrhardtoideae, Bambusoideae, Pooideae, and Panicoideae." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-997626578" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phylogeny, WGD, and gene families of Tibetan hulless barley compared with other plant genomes. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) The divergence time tree estimated on fourfold degenerate sites of single-copy orthologous genes. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) WGD and divergence of Tibetan hulless barley indicated by 4DTv (deviation at the fourfold degenerate third codon position) distribution. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Chromosomal syntenic relationship between Tibetan hulless barley and wheat A- and D-genome progenitors. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Gene families of &lt;em&gt;H. vulgare&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;T. urartu&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;T. aestivum&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Ae.tauschii&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;B. distachyon&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;E&lt;/em&gt;) Gene families of &lt;em&gt;Ehrhardtoideae, Bambusoideae&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Pooideae&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;Panicoideae&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F2.medium.gif" width="383" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F2.medium.gif" width="383" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/482107" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">Phylogeny, WGD, and gene families of Tibetan hulless barley compared with other plant genomes. (<em>A</em>) The divergence time tree estimated on fourfold degenerate sites of single-copy orthologous genes. (<em>B</em>) WGD and divergence of Tibetan hulless barley indicated by 4DTv (deviation at the fourfold degenerate third codon position) distribution. (<em>C</em>) Chromosomal syntenic relationship between Tibetan hulless barley and wheat A- and D-genome progenitors. (<em>D</em>) Gene families of <em>H. vulgare</em>, <em>T. urartu</em>, <em>T. aestivum</em>, <em>Ae.tauschii</em>, and <em>B. distachyon</em>. (<em>E</em>) Gene families of <em>Ehrhardtoideae, Bambusoideae</em>, <em>Pooideae</em>, and <em>Panicoideae</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-17">We compared gene families among barley and other Poaceae genomes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S16 and Table S26</a>). A total of 18,849 gene families are found in the Tibetan hulless barley genome, which is similar to those of <em>Ae. tauschii</em>, <em>T. urartu</em>, and <em>B. distachyon</em>. Within the Pooideae subfamily of Poaceae, 9,467 gene families are shared by five species, which is 40∼53% of the total number, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>D</em></a>). For pairwise comparison, more than 60% of families are shared between any two species. These results reflect common origin and genome divergency among Poaceae genomes. For intersubfamilies comparison, 11,132 are shared by four subfamilies—Pooideae, Panicoideae, Ehrhardtoideae, and Bambusoideae—contributing to ∼84% of Bambusoideae and ∼36% of Pooideae, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>E</em></a>).</p><p id="p-18">We also analyzed the expansion and contraction of gene families excluding the hexaploid wheat. Compared with the common ancestor of the Triticeae tribe, we found 2,185 expanded families in barley, but only 510 in the <em>Ae. tauschii</em>–<em>T. urartu</em> branch (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S16</a>). Among the 104 families significantly expanded in the barley lineage (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.05), the overrepresented gene ontology (GO) terms are mainly related to gene regulation and stress resistance, such as regulation of transcription, transcription factor activity, defense response, response to stress, etc. (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S27</a>). For example, we found more ethylene responsive factor (ERF) and dehydration-responsive element binding (DREB) transcription factors than those of <em>T. urartu</em> (2.0-fold), <em>Ae. tauschii</em> (1.6-fold), and Morex genomes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S28</a>). Extensive study revealed sound functions of both families in the regulation of responses to a wide range of abiotic and biotic stresses such as cold, drought, high salinity, heat shock, submergence, and multiple plant diseases, which made them ideal candidates for crop tolerance engineering (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>, <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). Furthermore, using functionally known genes as queries (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>), we found 230 cold-acclimated related genes in Tibetan hulless barley, which is also more abundant than those in <em>Ae. tauschii</em>, <em>T. urartu</em>, or other sequenced plant genomes analyzed in this study (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S28</a>).</p><p id="p-19">Analysis of the nonsynonymous to synonymous substitution ratio (Ka/Ks) among related plants found that the evolutionary process of Tibetan hulless barley diverged from Morex with more positively selected genes (Ka/Ks &gt; 1) than those from other species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S17</a>). KEGG pathway analysis indicated that many of the positively selected genes are involved in pathways related to environmental responses and adaptation, such as plant hormone signal transduction, replication and repair, plant–pathogen interaction, circadian rhythm–plant, calcium signaling pathway, etc. (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S29</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Genetic Diversity Among Wild and Cultivated Barleys.</h3><p id="p-20">To understand the genetic diversity in Tibetan barley germplasms, we carried out whole-genome resequencing of 10 strains representing wild and cultivated accessions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S30</a>). Genome alignment averaged 96.5% sequencing coverage and a 16.4-fold depth for each individual. A total of 36,469,491 single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and 2,281,198 small insertions and deletions were identified in individuals. For the wild group, 34,064,490 SNPs were detected, nearly twice of that found in cultivated hulless barleys (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S31 and S32</a> and <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). The genome-wide pairwise nucleotide diversity within population θ<sub>π</sub> and Watterson’s estimator of segregating sites θ<sub>w</sub> were 8.27 × 10<sup>−4</sup> and 7.17 × 10<sup>−4</sup> for wild and 3.31 × 10<sup>−4</sup> and 2.78 × 10<sup>−4</sup> for cultivated accessions, respectively. The principal component analysis (PCA) (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>) and STRUCTURE (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>) also indicated that the cultivated accessions cluster closely and wild groups are more divergent (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S18</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Population genetics of barleys. (A) SNP rate distribution for Tibetan wild barleys (W) and Tibetan hulless barley (C) across the seven chromosomes. There are 10 individuals including five Tibetan wild barleys and Tibetan hulless barleys. (B) STRUCTURE analysis of the 10 barley individuals. W1&#x2013;W5 refer to five wild samples, and C1&#x2013;C5 refer to five cultivated samples. (C) The neighbor-joining tree of barleys. The green lineages refer to six non-Tibetan barleys. The blue lineages refer to five Tibetan wild barleys. The red lineages refer to five Tibetan hulless barleys. (D) The adaptive correlation of randomly selected genes from SI Appendix, Table S34 (A1&#x2013;A50) with 10 stressful environmental variables (B1&#x2013;B10). The ecologically stressful variables include salinity, oxygen (low and high), solar radiation (especially high), CO2, drought, temperature (high and low), day length (short or long), and dormancy. The A1&#x2013;A50 genes demonstrating adaptive correlation with environmental stress are directly or indirectly affecting other genes or gene networks related to metabolite cycles and other biological vital functions. Direct effects result in high correlations; indirect effects result in low correlations." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-997626578" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Population genetics of barleys. The green lineages refer to six non-Tibetan barleys. The blue lineages refer to five Tibetan wild barleys. The red lineages refer to five Tibetan hulless barleys. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) The adaptive correlation of randomly selected genes from &lt;em&gt;SI Appendix&lt;/em&gt;, Table S34 (A1&#x2013;A50) with 10 stressful environmental variables (B1&#x2013;B10). <li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/4/1095/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/482038" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">Population genetics of barleys. (<em>A</em>) SNP rate distribution for Tibetan wild barleys (W) and Tibetan hulless barley (C) across the seven chromosomes. There are 10 individuals including five Tibetan wild barleys and Tibetan hulless barleys. (<em>B</em>) STRUCTURE analysis of the 10 barley individuals. W1–W5 refer to five wild samples, and C1–C5 refer to five cultivated samples. (<em>C</em>) The neighbor-joining tree of barleys. The green lineages refer to six non-Tibetan barleys. The blue lineages refer to five Tibetan wild barleys. The red lineages refer to five Tibetan hulless barleys. (<em>D</em>) The adaptive correlation of randomly selected genes from <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S34</a> (A1–A50) with 10 stressful environmental variables (B1–B10). The ecologically stressful variables include salinity, oxygen (low and high), solar radiation (especially high), CO<sub>2</sub>, drought, temperature (high and low), day length (short or long), and dormancy. The A1–A50 genes demonstrating adaptive correlation with environmental stress are directly or indirectly affecting other genes or gene networks related to metabolite cycles and other biological vital functions. Direct effects result in high correlations; indirect effects result in low correlations.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-22">We further investigated the genomic divergence between barley accessions of the Tibetan Plateau (Tibetan group) and six additional barley accessions previously sequenced (non-Tibetan group) (<a id="xref-ref-11-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). From the PCA (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S19</a>) and neighbor-joining tree (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a>), the Tibetan group and non-Tibetan group could be distinctly divided. The analysis of the population structure showed that a clear evolutionary divergence was evident between two groups (K = 2). When K &gt; 2, considerable genetic composition was observed to be shared by wild and cultivated accessions within the Tibetan group but is distinct from the non-Tibetan group (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S20</a>).</p><p id="p-23">The genome regions under selective sweeps due to the plateau environment were also investigated. As the measures of genetic differentiation of different populations, Tajima’s D (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>) and F<sub>st</sub> (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>) were introduced in this study. By comparison of two barley groups, the genome regions with F<sub>st</sub> &gt; 0.5 (or 5% top F<sub>st</sub> windows) between the Tibetan group and non-Tibetan groups and Tajima’s D &lt; –2 within the Tibetan group were determined as under selective sweeps. As a result, 1.07% of the genome and 1.23% (418) of the annotated genes were identified to be involved in selection (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S33 and S34</a>). KEGG analyses showed that some genes are enriched in pathways of plant hormone signal transduction, plant–pathogen interaction, phenylpropanoid biosynthesis, flavonoid biosynthesis, etc. (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S35</a>). Adaptive correlations of 50 randomly chosen genes with 10 stressful environmental variables, including salinity, oxygen (low and high), solar radiation (especially high), CO<sub>2</sub>, drought, temperature (high and low), day length (short or long), and dormancy, were analyzed (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S36</a>). A considerable number of these genes are found to be directly or indirectly associated with environmental stress adaptation (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>D</em></a>).</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-5"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-24" class="flushleft">Increasing evidence supports the Tibetan Plateau as one of the centers for cultivated barley domestication. In this study, we provide a draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley. This genome assembly is 3.89 Gb, accounting for about 87% of the whole genome, with high gene space coverage and high single-base accuracy. A genomic frame has been built by anchoring more than 89.4% of the assembly, comprising 33,928 out of 36,151 predicted genes, onto seven chromosomes. These data allow us to reinspect the divergent time between barley and other important Poaceae crops, especially species of the Triticeae tribe, by whole genome-wide in-depth analyses, as well as the WGD, synteny relationship, chromosome rearrangements, and gene families. These results not only present an exhaustive delineation of the Poaceae families’ evolution but also build substantial genomic groundwork and a good reference for marker development and gene identification in Triticeae crops with huge and repetitive genomes.</p><p id="p-25">The high genome coverage resequencing of 10 Tibetan wild and cultivated barley generated robust numbers of SNPs, among which the wild group contains nearly twice the SNP number than that of the cultivated group. θ<sub>π</sub>, θ<sub>w</sub>, and Tajima’s D values also indicate the significantly lower genetic diversity in cultivated accessions, reflecting the genetic bottlenecks introduced during domestication. Although the divergences between the Tibetan wild and cultivated barley are evident, they are even closer in comparison with barley accessions from Europe, East Asia, and Israel, which are sequenced by IBSC. This result came from analyses of PCA, STRUCTURE, F<sub>st</sub>, and cluster and is consistent with that obtained by transcriptome sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). Therefore, introducing the gene pool of Tibetan wild barley and those from non-Tibetan groups into Tibetan hulless barley may be helpful in widening the genetic background of Tibetan hulless barley.</p><p id="p-26">To adapt to the harsh environments of the plateau, Tibetan hulless barley may have been domesticated under distinct processes of natural selection compared with the cultivated barley from other parts. Therefore, it will be interesting to uncover evolutionary evidence for its adaptation by comparative analyses. From a gene family comparison with <em>Ae. tauschii</em>–<em>T. urartu</em>, we found significant gene family expansion in the barley lineage (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.05) with overrepresenting GO terms of regulation of transcription, transcription factor activity, defense response, etc. (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S27</a>). This may enable Tibetan hulless barley more flexibility to regulate its adaptation to extreme environmental challenges at high altitudes. For example, the ERF and DREB transcription factors that related to biotic and biotic stresses (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>, <a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>) and cold-acclimated related genes (<a id="xref-ref-7-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>) were expanded in the Tibetan hulless barley lineage (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S28</a>).</p><p id="p-27">Moreover, Ka/Ks analysis showed more positively selected genes between Tibetan hulless barley and Morex (Ka/Ks &gt; 1) than between other species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S17</a>). A considerable number of these genes are involved in pathways related to environmental responses and adaptation, such as plant hormone signal transduction, replication and repair, plant–pathogen interaction, etc. (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S29</a>). Similar results come from the selective sweep analyses between Tibetan and non-Tibetan barley. The adaptive correlation of individual genes with extensive stressful environmental variables was demonstrated. They are enriched in pathways related to environmental adaptation or stress response. For example, phenylpropanoid biosynthesis and flavonoid biosynthesis are essential for accumulation of chemical sunscreens in protecting against ultraviolet (UV) radiation in plants (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>). Plant hormone signal transduction pathways are not only important for germination, growth, and development, but also play key roles in responses to biotic and abiotic stress. Intriguingly, almost 45% of selected regions were linked to chromosome 7H, which indicated that more adaptation-related genes may lie on the chromosome that undergoes more severe selection. The selected regions provide targets for identification of adaptation-related genes from Tibetan barley. For example, a previously known drought stress-related quantitative trait locus (QTL), QRwc.TaEr-7H.2, which is associated with leaf relative water content, was found in selective sweep regions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1423628112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1423628112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S21</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>). These results will facilitate crop improvement toward sustainable supplies of food, feed, and industry resources for people living on the highland and around the world.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-6"><h2 class="">Methods</h2><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Sequencing and Assembly.</h3><p id="p-28">Genomic DNA was isolated from a landrace of Tibetan hulless barley <em>L. goumang</em>. Paired-end sequencing (PE151, PE101, PE91, and PE50) was performed on the Illumina HiSeq2000 platform for short insert size libraries including 250, 500, and 800 base pairs (bps) and mate-pair libraries of 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb, and 40 kb. A stringent filtering process on raw reads was done to remove low quality, adapter contamination, small insert, and PCR duplicated reads. Short insert size data were further corrected by the <em>ErrorCorrection</em> module in <em>SOAPdenovo2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). When conducting the assembly using <em>SOAPdenovo2</em>, we constructed contigs using corrected short insert size data with the K-mer parameter set to 67 and built a scaffold using all paired-end clean data. We used <em>SSPACE-V1.1</em> (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>) to construct a superscaffold with all mate-pair clean data. <em>GapCloser</em> (<a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>) was used to fill gaps within the scaffold to improve the contig N50 length.</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Genome Assembly Evaluation.</h3><p id="p-29">We mapped the assembled Tibetan hulless barley genome sequences—10 selected BAC sequences from a paper published by IBSC (<em>BlastN</em>, –e 1e-5; nucleotide identity, &gt;0.97)—to check the quality and coverage of our assembly. We aligned 24.5-fold of clean data to BAC sequences by <em>SOAPaligner</em> (<a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>) to observe the sequencing coverage depth across BAC sequences. The gene region coverage rate was calculated using <em>BLAT</em> (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>), aligning Tibetan hulless barley scaffolds to <em>Trinity</em> (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>)-assembled unigenes to determine the percentage of unigene bases covered by our assembly.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Repeat Annotation and Gene Annotation.</h3><p id="p-30">Genome sequences were scanned for known repeats by <em>RepeatMasker</em> and <em>ProteinRepeatMask</em> against <em>Repbase</em> (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). <em>RepeatModeler</em> (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>) and <em>LTR-FINDER</em> (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>), de novo prediction programs, were used to build the de novo repeat library based on the genome; then, contamination and multicopy genes in the library were removed. <em>LTR_FINDER</em> was used to search the whole genome for characteristic structures of the full-length LTRs (its ∼18-bp sequence was complementary to the 3′ tail of some tRNAs). Then, our in-house pipeline was used to filter the low-quality and falsely predicted LTRs. Using this library as a database, <em>RepeatMasker</em> was run to find and classify the repeats. Gene models were predicted using de novo software such as <em>AUGUSTUS</em> (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) and <em>GENSCAN</em> (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>); homolog-based methods to map <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, <em>B. distachyon</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>Sorghum bicolor</em>, and <em>Zea mays</em> protein sequences to Tibetan hulless barley genome sequences using <em>TBlastN</em> (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>) and <em>GENEWISE</em> (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>) software to infer gene structure; an RNA-seq–based method to map transcriptome data to the reference genome using <em>TopHat</em> (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>); and assembling transcripts with <em>Cufflinks</em> (<a id="xref-ref-39-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>) to obtain the gene structure. Finally, <em>GLEAN</em> (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>) was used to make the high-confidence gene model by combining all of the evidence. Predicted protein-coding genes were further assigned functions by aligning them to the best matches in the <em>SwissProt</em> and <em>TrEMBL</em> databases (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>), determining the motifs and domains using <em>InterProScan</em> (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>), and assigning <em>Gene Ontology</em> (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>) and KEGG pathway (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>) annotations.</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Chromosome Reconstruction Based on Barley Physical and Genetic Frameworks.</h3><p id="p-31">The Tibetan hulless barley scaffold sequences were aligned to the IBSC barley physical map sequences anchored to the high-resolution genetic map using <em>BlastN</em> (length, ≥200 bp; e-value, ≤1e<sup>−5</sup>; identity, ≥99%). Data for the IBSC barley physical map were downloaded from <a href="ftp://ftpmips.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plants/barley/public_data/anchoring/" class="in-nw">ftp://ftpmips.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plants/barley/public_data/anchoring/</a>. The Tibetan hulless barley genome sequences were mapped to the IBSC barley sequences to determine their location in the genetic map. The best scoring matches were selected in the case of multiple matches.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Collinearity Among Poaceae Species.</h3><p id="p-32">We performed all-versus-all <em>BlastP</em> (e-value, &lt;1e-5) and then detected syntenic blocks using <em>MCscan</em>. The 4DTv values (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>) (deviation at the fourfold degenerate third codon position) among paralogous gene pairs of Tibetan hulless barley were calculated and revised in the Hasegawa, Kishino and Yano (HKY) model to analyze WGD events. The chromosomal collinearity of Poaceae species based on orthologous gene pairs was drawn among the species. The nested chromosomal fusion events were deduced by genome synteny between Tibetan hulless barley chromosomes and rice chromosomes.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Phylogenetic Analysis.</h3><p id="p-33">We constructed gene families for thirteen species including <em>Hordeum vulgare</em>, <em>Triticum urartu</em>, <em>Triticum aestivum</em>, <em>Aegilops tauschii</em>, <em>Brachypodium distachyon</em>, <em>Phyllostachys heterocycla</em>, <em>Oryza sativa</em>, <em>Sorghum bicolor</em>, <em>Zea mays</em>, <em>Senna italic</em>, <em>Carica papaya</em>, <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, and <em>Vitis vinifera</em> with <em>OrthoMCL</em> (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>) methods on the all-versus-all <em>BlastP</em> alignment (e-value, &lt;1e-5). Single-copy ortholog protein sequences for each species were aligned by multiple sequence comparison by log-expectation (MUSCLE) (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>), and the corresponding CDS sequences were concatenated to supergene sequences. To build a species phylogenetic tree by <em>PhyML</em> (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>) under the GTR+gamma model with approximate likelihood ratio test (aLRT) assessment of branch reliability, 4D-sites were extracted. Divergence times were estimated using the Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood (<em>PAML</em>) <em>mcmctree</em> (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>) program with the approximate likelihood calculation method, and the convergence was checked by <em>Tracer</em> (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>) and confirmed by two independent runs.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3 class="">Genome Diversity of Cultivars and Wild Barleys.</h3><p id="p-34">Ten barley accessions, including five cultivars and five wild barleys, were each sequenced for more than 15-fold in PE91. The clean reads were mapped to reference Tibetan hulless barley genomes by Burrows–Wheeler alignment tool (BWA) (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). We then used the Genome Analysis Toolkit (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>) to identify SNP and insertions and deletions. PCA and population structure analysis were performed by <em>EIGENSOFT</em> (<a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>) and <em>FRAPPE</em> (<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), respectively. Genetic diversity of cultivars and wild barleys were compared by calculating the SNP rate in 500-kb windows across the genome.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-35">We thank M. C. Luo (Department of Plant Sciences, University of California) for his valuable suggestions on data analyses. This work was supported by the following funding sources: the Tibet Autonomous Region Financial Special Fund (2011XZCZZX001), the National Science and Technology Support Program (2012BAD03B01), and the National Program on Key Basic Research Project (2011CB111512, 2012CB723006).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-3"><p id="p-3"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-3-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>X.Z., H.L., Z.W., S.Z., Y.T., Z.H., and Y.W. contributed equally to this work.</p></li><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>2</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed.
